A Texas Blue Bonnet

CHAPTER I
BLUE BONNET

Blue Bonnet came up the steps of the long, low ranch house, and threw herself listlessly back in one of the deep veranda chairs.

‚ÄúTired, Honey?‚Äù Mr. Ashe asked, laying down his paper.

‚ÄúYes, Uncle Cliff. I—hate walking!‚Äù

‚ÄúThen why not ride?‚Äù

Blue Bonnet was smoothing the ears of Don, the big collie who had followed her up on to the veranda, and now stood resting his fine head on her knee. ‚ÄúI—didn‚Äôt want to,‚Äù she answered, slowly, without looking up.

‚ÄúSee here, Honey,‚Äù said Mr. Ashe, leaning toward her, a note of inquiry in his deep, pleasant voice; ‚Äúcome to think of it, you haven‚Äôt been riding lately.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo, Uncle Cliff.‚Äù Blue Bonnet‚Äôs eyes were turned now out over the wide stretch of prairie before the house.

‚ÄúAny reason, Honey?‚Äù

2 The girl hesitated. ‚ÄúYes, Uncle Cliff.‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you want to tell me it, Blue Bonnet?‚Äù

‚ÄúNo,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered, slowly, ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to tell it to you. I—it‚Äôs because I‚Äôm—afraid.‚Äù

‚ÄúAfraid! Blue Bonnet! That‚Äôs an odd word for an Ashe to use!‚Äù

‚ÄúI know, Uncle Cliff; I reckon I‚Äôm not an Ashe—clear through.‚Äù Blue Bonnet rose hurriedly and ran down the steps. Around the house she went, and in through the back way to her own room. There she brushed the hot tears from her eyes with an impatient movement. ‚ÄúOh, it is true,‚Äù she said to herself, ‚Äúand I can‚Äôt help it. Oh, if I could only go away—I hate it here! Hate it! Hate it!‚Äù

Later, swinging in the hammock on the back veranda, she looked up suddenly as her uncle came to sit on the railing beside her. Something in his face and manner made her wonder.

‚ÄúBlue Bonnet,‚Äù he said, abruptly, ‚Äúwe might as well have it out—right here and now—it‚Äôll be the best thing for us both.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet sat up, pushing back her soft, thick hair. ‚ÄúHave it out?‚Äù she repeated.

‚ÄúBlue Bonnet,‚Äù he answered, bending nearer, ‚Äúsuppose you tell me just what it is you would like to do? It wouldn‚Äôt take much insight to see that you aren‚Äôt very happy nowadays; and—well, I3 reckon your father wouldn‚Äôt want things going on as they‚Äôve been—lately.‚Äù

The girl‚Äôs face changed swiftly. ‚ÄúOh, I have been horrid, Uncle Cliff! But I—oh, I do so—hate it—here!‚Äù

‚ÄúHate it here! Hate the Blue Bonnet Ranch—the finest bit of country in the whole state of Texas!‚Äù

‚ÄúI—hate the whole state of Texas!‚Äù

‚ÄúBlue Bonnet!‚Äù

‚ÄúI do. I want to go East to live. I—my mother was an Easterner. I want to live her life.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut, Honey, your mother chose to come West. Why, child,‚Äù—there was a quick note of triumph in the man‚Äôs voice—‚Äúit was your mother who named you Blue Bonnet.‚Äù

‚ÄúI wish she hadn‚Äôt. It‚Äôs a—ridiculous sort of name—I would like to have been called Elizabeth—it is my name, too.‚Äù

‚ÄúElizabeth?‚Äù Mr. Ashe repeated. ‚ÄúIt doesn‚Äôt seem to suit you nearly as well, Honey. All the same, if you like it. But Blue—Elizabeth, you know that this is your ranch, and that your father wanted you brought up to know all about it, so as to be able to manage things for yourself a bit—at a pinch.‚Äù

‚ÄúI shall sell—as soon as I come of age.‚Äù

Mr. Ashe rose. ‚ÄúI reckon we‚Äôd best not talk any more now.‚Äù

4 ‚ÄúUncle Clifford.‚Äù Blue Bonnet looked up. ‚ÄúUncle Clifford, please don‚Äôt think it‚Äôs just—temper. I mean it, truly—I sha‚Äôn‚Äôt ever make a Westerner. I‚Äôm sorry—on your account. Still, it‚Äôs true—I hate it all—now,—everything the life out here stands for—and I want to go East. I—I don‚Äôt see why I shouldn‚Äôt choose my own life—for myself.‚Äù

Her uncle looked down into the upturned, eager face. ‚ÄúYou seem to have gone over this pretty thoroughly in your own mind, Bl—Elizabeth.‚Äù

‚ÄúI have, Uncle Cliff.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, you and I‚Äôll talk things over another time; I‚Äôve some business to see to now. I suppose things‚Äôll have to go on, even if you do intend to sell—in six years.‚Äù

‚ÄúI wish you‚Äôd try to see my side of it, Uncle Cliff.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm going to—after a while. Just now, I can‚Äôt get beyond the fact that you hate the Blue Bonnet Ranch. I hope your father doesn‚Äôt know it!‚Äù And Mr. Ashe turned away.

Below the house, leaning against the low fence enclosing the oblong piece of ground called ‚Äúthe garden,‚Äù Mr. Ashe found Uncle Joe Terry, ranch foreman, and his chief adviser in the difficult task of bringing up his orphan niece.

Uncle Joe was smoking placidly, his eyes on the wild riot of color which was one of the principal5 characteristics of Blue Bonnet‚Äôs garden. ‚ÄúTell you what,‚Äù he said, as Mr. Ashe came up, ‚Äúthis here place needs weeding. Blue Bonnet ain‚Äôt been keeping an eye on Miguel lately.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet‚Äôs uncle stood a moment looking down at the neglected garden. ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúand it‚Äôs not only the garden, Joe, that‚Äôs been left to itself lately.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe ain‚Äôt been out on Firefly this two weeks,‚Äù Uncle Joe commented. ‚ÄúWhat‚Äôs wrong, Cliff?‚Äù

‚ÄúShe wants to go East.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo that‚Äôs it? Well, I reckon it‚Äôs natural—wants to run with the other young folks, I suppose?‚Äù

‚ÄúBut—Joe, she says she hates—the ranch.‚Äù

Uncle Joe puffed at his pipe thoughtfully. ‚ÄúHm—so she says that? She always was an outspoken little piece, Cliff.‚Äù

‚ÄúShe says, too, that she means to sell.‚Äù

‚ÄúMy lady must be a bit excited. Well, it won‚Äôt be to-morrow, Cliff, and a whole lot of things can happen in six years. You just give my lady her head; she‚Äôs looking to be crossed, and she‚Äôs all braced up to pull the other way. All you want to do is to go with her a bit.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a pretty big proposition—sending her East,‚Äù Mr. Ashe said. ‚ÄúOh, she‚Äôll pick up a lot of tomfool notions, most likely,‚Äù Uncle Joe admitted, ‚Äúand a whole heap of others that‚Äôll come in6 mighty handy one of these days. You just send her ‚Äôlong back to those folks of her mother‚Äôs and quit worrying.‚Äù

That night Mr. Ashe wrote a letter to Blue Bonnet‚Äôs grandmother. He said nothing to Blue Bonnet herself about it, however. Possibly Mrs. Clyde would not care to assume the charge of her granddaughter. In any case, it would be well to have the matter settled before mentioning it.

Then one evening, not a fortnight later, Uncle Joe, coming home from the little post-office town, twenty miles away, tossed him several letters.

‚ÄúPostmarked Woodford,‚Äù the older man said. ‚ÄúLooks like sentence was about to be pronounced.‚Äù

Five minutes more and Mr. Ashe knew how hard he had been hoping against hope these last two weeks.

‚ÄúWell?‚Äù Uncle Joe asked; and the other looked up to find him still sitting motionless in his saddle.

‚ÄúThey want her to come as soon as possible, so that she may be ready to start school at the beginning of the fall term.‚Äù

‚ÄúPretty good school back there?‚Äù

‚ÄúSaid to be—it‚Äôs the one her mother went to.‚Äù

‚ÄúI reckon they‚Äôre tickled to death to have her come?‚Äù

‚ÄúThey seem pleased.‚Äù

‚ÄúBlue Bonnet‚Äôs out in the garden,‚Äù Uncle Joe suggested.

7 Blue Bonnet was gathering nasturtiums when her uncle called to her from the gate at the upper end of the garden. He had two letters in his hand, and, as she reached him, he held them out. ‚ÄúThey came to-night,‚Äù he explained. ‚ÄúThey are in answer to one I wrote a short time ago.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet took them wonderingly, and, sitting on the ground, the great bunch of gay-colored nasturtiums beside her, she opened one of them. As it happened, it was the one from her Aunt Lucinda—a short letter, perfectly kind and sincere, but very formal. On the whole, a rather depressing letter, in spite of the answer it brought to her great desire.

Blue Bonnet refolded it rather soberly. ‚ÄúI wish,‚Äù she said, studying the firm, upright handwriting, ‚Äúthat I hadn‚Äôt read this one first. Grandmother‚Äôs must be different.‚Äù

It certainly was. A letter overflowing with the joy the writer felt over the prospect of Blue Bonnet‚Äôs coming. Through its magic the girl was carried far away from the little garden, from all the old familiar scenes. Dimly remembered stories her mother used to tell her of the big white house standing amidst its tall trees came back to her, and the vague hopes and dreams that had been filling her thoughts for weeks past began to take definite form.

And she was going there—back to her mother‚Äôs old home. She was to have the very room that had8 been her mother‚Äôs,—Grandmother had said so. It seemed too good to be true. She was glad, now, she had kept this letter to the last. And she would be going soon;—that thought, with its accompanying one of hurry and preparation, brought her back to the present.

Picking up the letters, she ran up to the house. On the back steps she found Uncle Joe.

‚ÄúSeems like you was in a hurry,‚Äù he said.

Blue Bonnet laughed, looking at him with shining eyes. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm going East!‚Äù

‚ÄúTo-night?‚Äù he questioned.

‚ÄúNo, not to-night; but very soon, I think.‚Äù

Uncle Joe seemed neither surprised, nor impressed. ‚ÄúHumph,‚Äù he grunted, knocking the ashes from his pipe. ‚ÄúWell, I reckon it‚Äôs all right back East—for them that like it.‚Äù

His reception of her news rather daunted Blue Bonnet, and she went at a slower pace through the wide center hall to the front veranda, where her uncle sat.

‚ÄúUncle Cliff,‚Äù she asked, giving him the letters, ‚Äúyou mean—I‚Äôm to go?‚Äù

Mr. Ashe shifted the letters from one hand to the other for a moment, without speaking; then he said gravely, ‚ÄúYes, you‚Äôre to go, Elizabeth. When a girl hates the ranch, hates everything the life here stands for, and is afraid to ride, I don‚Äôt see that there‚Äôs anything left to do—but send her East.‚Äù

9 Blue Bonnet dropped down on the upper step, the quick color flooding her face. To go East was one thing—but to be sent! She sat very still for a few moments, looking out over the broad, level prairie.

Her uncle was the first to speak.

‚ÄúI suppose you‚Äôd best get started pretty soon; there‚Äôll be some fixing up to do after you get there.‚Äù

‚ÄúAm I going alone?‚Äù Blue Bonnet asked.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt see how I can leave home at present,‚Äù her uncle answered. ‚ÄúPerhaps I‚Äôll hear of some one going East who‚Äôll be willing to look after you.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôll seem funny to go to school with other girls,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said. ‚ÄúI wonder how I‚Äôll like going to school.‚Äù

‚ÄúI reckon you‚Äôll be learning a good many lessons of various kinds, Honey.‚Äù Mr. Ashe spoke a little wistfully. It was hard to realize that Blue Bonnet was going away.

The girl looked up soberly; his words had somehow reminded her of Aunt Lucinda‚Äôs letter. A sudden dread of the writer of it seized her. ‚ÄúUncle Cliff,‚Äù she asked, ‚Äúwhat are they like—Grandmother and Aunt Lucinda?‚Äù

‚ÄúSuppose you wait and find out for yourself, Honey.‚Äù

‚ÄúI wish Aunt Lucinda hadn‚Äôt been so much older than Mamma. Uncle Cliff, have you ever been in Woodford?‚Äù

10 ‚ÄúNo, Honey; it‚Äôs a right pretty place, I reckon. You‚Äôll have to write and tell me all about it.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd you‚Äôll answer, won‚Äôt you? You‚Äôll write very often?‚Äù

‚ÄúOf course, Honey; but I don‚Äôt know what I‚Äôll find to tell you—you won‚Äôt care about ranch talk.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut you‚Äôll write? You‚Äôve promised—and you‚Äôve never broken a promise to me,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said.

And that night, lying awake and thinking of the new life to come, Blue Bonnet found the thought of those promised letters strangely comforting. ‚ÄúIt—it can‚Äôt seem so far then,‚Äù she told herself.

‚ÄúHurry, Benita!‚Äù Blue Bonnet urged, ‚ÄúI hear Uncle Joe coming.‚Äù

The old woman gave a finishing touch to the waist she was laying in place in the big trunk standing in the center of Blue Bonnet‚Äôs room. ‚ÄúSi, Se√±orita,‚Äù she said, ‚Äúall is ready.‚Äù

She lifted the tray in place and closed down the lid, passing a hand admiringly over the surface of the trunk. ‚ÄúSe√±orita has the trunk of the Se√±ora, is it not?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered gravely.

‚ÄúI remember, as it were but yesterday, the coming of the Se√±ora,‚Äù Benita said, ‚Äúand the Se√±or calling ‚ÄòBenita! Oh, Benita! Here is your new mistress!‚Äô She was but the young thing—that11 little Se√±ora—not much older than you are now, Se√±orita mia, and with the face all bright and the eyes so expressive—like yours.‚Äù

‚ÄúEighteen,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said, thoughtfully, ‚Äúand I‚Äôm fifteen.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt was I who unpacked the trunk—this and others, for there were many—and now I am packing it again for the going of the Se√±orita.‚Äù Benita‚Äôs voice was trembling. ‚ÄúAnd the Se√±orita goes to the home of her mother‚Äôs mother. Much would the Se√±ora tell me of the home she had left, in those first days.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet came to put an arm about the old woman, who, since her mother‚Äôs death ten years before, had mothered and looked after her to the best of her ability. ‚ÄúI wish you were going too, Benita,‚Äù she said.

‚ÄúSi, Se√±orita mia, it is the journey too long for old Benita.‚Äù

‚ÄúAll the way from Texas to Massachusetts,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said. ‚ÄúI wonder who‚Äôll look after me and do everything for me there, Benita.‚Äù

‚ÄúThat thought troubles me much, also, Se√±orita.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, I‚Äôll get along somehow,‚Äù Blue Bonnet laughed. She turned as Uncle Joe came down the hall, a coil of rope over his shoulder.

‚ÄúReady!‚Äù she called.

‚ÄúThis looks like business, for sure,‚Äù Uncle Joe12 said, slipping an end of the rope under Blue Bonnet‚Äôs trunk.

She nodded rather soberly. She had worn a sober face a good deal of the time during the days of preparation. ‚ÄúUncle Joe,‚Äù—she looked up a little wistfully into the kind, weather-beaten face,—‚Äúyou—you‚Äôll look after Uncle Cliff, won‚Äôt you?‚Äù

‚ÄúSure I will, Blue Bonnet, same‚Äôs if he was an infant in arms.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd you‚Äôll write to me, too, sometimes—and tell me all about—everything?‚Äù

‚ÄúI ain‚Äôt much on letter-writing,‚Äù Uncle Joe answered, ‚Äúbut I‚Äôll make a try at it now and then; and you‚Äôre going to be so busy doing the things you‚Äôre wanting to do that you won‚Äôt have much time to be pestered with the goings-on out here.‚Äù

‚ÄúPlease, Uncle Joe, you know that isn‚Äôt so.‚Äù

‚ÄúAin‚Äôt it? There now, that‚Äôs roped to stay. Seems kind of hard to realize that come another twenty-four hours and the Blue Bonnet Ranch‚Äôll be without its best and prettiest Blue Bonnet. Eh, Benita?‚Äù

Benita shook her gray head sadly. ‚ÄúThe sunshine goes with the going of the Se√±orita,‚Äù she said.

‚ÄúI reckon you‚Äôll take to the doings back there all right, Blue Bonnet,‚Äù Uncle Joe began. ‚ÄúThere! I‚Äôm always forgetting—just as if your uncle hadn‚Äôt13 explained how, seeing as everything was to be new, you wasn‚Äôt to be Blue Bonnet any more, but Elizabeth. It‚Äôs a fine name, Elizabeth, and it‚Äôs going to suit back East all right; but, if you was staying on here, I‚Äôm thinking you‚Äôd have to go on being Blue Bonnet. I doubt if the boys here on the ranch would stand for anything else—they‚Äôre sort of kicking now over your going.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said, ‚ÄúI‚Äôve had to say such a lot of good-byes—I don‚Äôt see why they care so much.‚Äù And, after Uncle Joe had carried out the trunk, and Benita had gone, she sat quite still on the foot of her bed beside her half-packed hand-bag, trying to realize that in another twenty-four hours she would be travelling further and further from the Blue Bonnet Ranch.

She and her uncle were to leave early the next morning, taking the long drive to the nearest railway station in the cool of the day. Mr. Ashe was to go the first hundred miles with her, and from there on she would be in charge of a friend of his who was going East.

And she had never been fifty miles on the railway in her life! Blue Bonnet‚Äôs eyes brightened. She drew a quick breath of pleasure. To be fifteen, and setting out to the land of one‚Äôs heart‚Äôs desire! All the doubts, the regrets, the half-vague fears of the past ten days vanished.

Hearing her uncle‚Äôs step on the veranda, she went14 out to meet him. He was looking down at the trunk; something of the same expression in his eyes that had been in old Benita‚Äôs.

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you wish you were going, too?‚Äù the girl asked gaily.

‚ÄúYes, Honey.‚Äù

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt it a big trunk and doesn‚Äôt it look delightfully travellingified?‚Äù

‚ÄúDelightfully what?‚Äù

Blue Bonnet laughed. Reaching up, she touched the little knot of dark blue, pea-like blossoms in her uncle‚Äôs buttonhole. ‚ÄúYou won‚Äôt forget me while you have your blue bonnets,‚Äù she said.

‚ÄúI reckon I won‚Äôt forget you, Honey.‚Äù

They went in to supper, Blue Bonnet talking and laughing excitedly; but afterwards, when she and her uncle went out to the front veranda as usual, her mood changed suddenly. It was so still, so peaceful, out there—and yet, already, so strangely alien.

For a few moments she walked up and down restlessly, followed closely by Don. Don scented the coming change; he thoroughly disapproved of that roped trunk on the back veranda.

‚ÄúUncle Cliff—‚Äù Blue Bonnet came at last to sit on the arm of her uncle‚Äôs chair, letting her head rest on his shoulder. Something had got to be put into words, which she had been trying to say in various other ways for a good many days past.15 ‚ÄúUncle Cliff, I—truly—I am sorry—that I spoke the way I did—that night.‚Äù

Mr. Ashe stroked the brown head gently. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs all right, Honey. And remember, Honey, if things go wrong, if you‚Äôre disappointed, or—anything like that, you‚Äôve only to send word. This is your home,—and will be—for six years. And, Honey, you won‚Äôt forget,—what your father said,—that you were to try to live as he had taught you to ride—straight and true.‚Äù

CHAPTER II
ELIZABETH

Blue Bonnet gathered up her belongings; ten minutes more and they would be in, the porter had told her.

Mr. Garner, her uncle‚Äôs friend, had brought her as far as New York; from there on she had travelled alone. Now that she was so near her journey‚Äôs end she almost wished she were not.

Aunt Lucinda was to meet her in Boston. Blue Bonnet gave her hair a smoothing touch or two and pulled on her gloves; then the porter came to brush her off, smiling sympathetically over her evident nervousness, and assuring her that Boston was ‚Äúa right fine place.‚Äù

Very crowded, very confusing she thought it, during those first few moments. Inside the car, people were beginning to gather up bundles and wraps; outside, as the train drew into the great depot, pandemonium seemed the order of the day. Blue Bonnet felt a sudden, overwhelming desire to break away; to get somewhere—anywhere, where it was quiet.

And then she saw Aunt Lucinda coming towards17 her. She knew instinctively that it was Aunt Lucinda the moment she caught sight of the tall, well-dressed woman threading her way down the crowded aisle.

‚ÄúThis is Elizabeth?‚Äù she said, stopping before Blue Bonnet.

The girl answered nervously that she supposed so. ‚ÄúYou see,‚Äù she added, quickly, flushing over the ridiculousness of her reply, ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not used to being called anything but Blue Bonnet.‚Äù

‚ÄúElizabeth, or Blue Bonnet, we are very glad you have come to us, my dear,‚Äù Miss Clyde answered, kissing her; ‚Äúit must have seemed a long way.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, Aunt Lucinda,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said. At that moment Texas seemed a very, very long way off, indeed. She followed her aunt down the aisle and out on to the busy platform, feeling curiously small and lonely.

During the short ride on the local train Blue Bonnet was very silent, but Miss Clyde thought her interested in the view from the car window and did not try to make conversation.

She was rather glad of the opportunity to study the slender, bright-faced girl opposite.

‚ÄúHow near everything is to everything else, Aunt Lucinda,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said at last.

Miss Clyde smiled. ‚ÄúWe don‚Äôt run much to space here, Elizabeth. There, that is our last stop18 before Woodford. You will be glad to have your long journey really over.‚Äù

At Woodford the old family carriage was waiting. Denham, the coachman, smiled welcomingly at Blue Bonnet. ‚Äú‚ÄôDeed and I‚Äôm glad to see Miss Elizabeth‚Äôs girl,‚Äù he said.

Blue Bonnet smiled back in friendly fashion. ‚ÄúDid he know Mamma, Aunt Lucinda?‚Äù she asked, wonderingly.

‚ÄúDenham has been with us for more than twenty years, Elizabeth,‚Äù Miss Clyde answered.

There were not many passengers for the sleepy little station. Blue Bonnet felt herself the object of interest for the group of loungers gathered about the platform.

To the girl the old tree-shaded village, with its air of quiet content, its one wide principal street, with pleasant by-ways straggling off at irregular intervals from it, was very attractive, and very interesting as well, when contrasted with the little bare prairie town at home. She quite enjoyed the slow, leisurely drive in the comfortable old carry-all; she could not imagine any one dashing up that sober quiet street. And when, at last, they turned into a broad, well-kept drive, and she caught sight, across the smooth stretch of green lawn, of the big white house, she drew a quick breath of content; it was all in such perfect keeping.

Miss Clyde saw the look in Blue Bonnet‚Äôs eyes19 and an answering smile showed in her own. ‚ÄúYour mother was very fond of the old place, Elizabeth,‚Äù she said; ‚Äúwe are very glad to have her daughter come home to it.‚Äù

On the steps Mrs. Clyde was waiting, and to her Blue Bonnet‚Äôs heart went out instantly.

‚ÄúAh, but you are like your mother, my dear!‚Äù Mrs. Clyde cried, holding the girl close. ‚ÄúIt is very good of your uncle to spare you to us. I could hardly believe the good news when it came. But you are tired, dear; you shall go to your room at once.‚Äù

‚ÄúI am tired,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said; she wondered why it was she wanted to cry. And why in this first moment of coming—coming home, Aunt Lucinda had called it—her thoughts kept going back to the home she had left.

She went with her aunt up the broad oak stairway and along the wide upper hall to a room at the lower end,—a big pleasant room,—the one that had been her mother‚Äôs. It was, indeed, a charming room, with its wide, cushioned window-seats, its deep, open fireplace, its pretty light furniture and delicate draperies. The windows looked off into orchard and garden, and, when Aunt Lucinda had gone downstairs again, Blue Bonnet went to kneel before the one overlooking the latter.

In a moment she had forgotten how tired and dusty she was; forgotten how far she had journeyed20 since the morning she said good-bye to Uncle Joe and old Benita and Don; had forgotten everything but the garden lying, half in shade, half in sunshine, below,—the big, rambling, old-fashioned garden, of which the one at home was a faint reproduction.

Beyond the garden was a tall row of trees, growing so closely together as to form a thick screen. Blue Bonnet wondered what was on the other side of that row? Did her grandmother‚Äôs land end on this side? Could there be neighbors so near?

She wondered a good deal about it as she freshened herself up for supper. Her trunk had not come yet, but she had a fresh white waist in her suit-case. Presently she came slowly along the hall and downstairs to where Mrs. Clyde was sitting in the broad entrance hall.

‚ÄúIt is very good to see a young person coming down those stairs again,‚Äù Mrs. Clyde said; ‚Äúyou come much more slowly than your mother used to, dear.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet smiled. ‚ÄúIt seems odd to be going up and coming down stairs at all. At home it is all on one floor.‚Äù She went to stand by the open front door. Across the lawn and the broad road beyond, she caught glimpses of other big white houses, behind their sheltering trees.

‚ÄúOh,‚Äù she said, ‚Äúif you only knew how delightful it seems to have real neighbors, Grandmother.21 At home our nearest neighbors were twenty miles away. I‚Äôve been so hungry for people, and houses, and everything.‚Äù

The next morning Blue Bonnet made her first acquaintance among her new neighbors. She had gone out to see for herself what lay beyond that tall screen of trees. Nothing at all mysterious, she found; merely another broad green lawn centering itself about an old creeper-covered brick house. Following the path beside the trees, she came to a low picket-fence, over which ran a stile. Blue Bonnet sat down on the upper step to survey at leisure this next-door place; and then she saw that from midway across the lawn some one was surveying her,—a boy of about her own age.

‚ÄúGood morning,‚Äù he said.

‚ÄúGood morning,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered. ‚ÄúDo you live here?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a very pretty place.‚Äù

The other turned to look back at the old house. ‚ÄúI suppose it is,‚Äù he admitted, ‚Äúthough I‚Äôve never thought much about it.‚Äù He came nearer, whistling to a pair of fox-terrier puppies, who were worrying at something at the further end of the lawn. ‚ÄúDo you like dogs?‚Äù he asked.

‚ÄúI adore them,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered.

‚ÄúBob and Ben are pretty decent little chaps,‚Äù the22 boy said, and he brought the dogs up to be introduced.

‚ÄúThey‚Äôre dears,‚Äù Blue Bonnet declared warmly, patting the two upturned heads.

Alec whistled. ‚ÄúAnd you had to come away and leave it all! What a shame!—but you‚Äôve got it to go back to—I wish I had!‚Äù

23 ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you like it here in Woodford?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs a poky old hole. You can‚Äôt throw a stone in any direction without breaking a window—or a tradition.‚Äù

Five minutes later, Blue Bonnet came slowly downstairs; pausing on the landing long enough to declare under her breath that she was perfectly sure she should hate Sarah Blake.

Sarah was waiting in the darkened front parlor. She was short and fair; rather unimaginative and30 decidedly conscientious. She very much disliked calling upon strangers, and for that reason had chosen the earliest opportunity to come and see Blue Bonnet.

‚ÄúHow do you do?‚Äù she said, as Blue Bonnet appeared. ‚ÄúMrs. Clyde asked me to come and see you. I hope you will like Woodford.‚Äù

It was a new philosophy for Sarah.

31 ‚ÄúAre all the girls here like that?‚Äù Blue Bonnet asked.

Blue Bonnet did know that verb; knew it in all its various moods and tenses with the thoroughness her aunt delighted in. ‚ÄúThat was very well done, Elizabeth,‚Äù she said.

And Blue Bonnet found the quiet words of commendation well worth while.

Conversation during dinner, led by Mrs. Clyde, concerned itself chiefly with the coming tea-party. Tea-parties were unknown things to Blue Bonnet. It seemed to her that they were rather serious affairs. Especially did it appear too bad to go to so much trouble for so few guests; and she could not get over her feeling of sympathy for those left out.

‚ÄúThese are the young girls from among whom your grandmother and I wish you to choose your friends, Elizabeth,‚Äù her aunt told her.

‚ÄúIt is not good taste to criticize your friends, Elizabeth.‚Äù

39 ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not sure she is going to be a friend, Aunt Lucinda.‚Äù

Thursday, the day set for the tea-party, was in Blue Bonnet‚Äôs estimation a perfect day. All the morning the outdoor world was calling to her, getting in return more than one involuntary response. About noontime, Alec came whistling up the back path, Bob and Ben at his heels. Blue Bonnet was on the steps studying.

‚ÄúBusy?‚Äù he asked.

Blue Bonnet threw down her book and caught up her shade hat from a nearby chair. ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs start right away!‚Äù

40 They went down the path to where a gate opened into a wide open meadow, Blue Bonnet whistling to Solomon as they went.

At the foot of the meadow lay the brook; a sunny, quiet enough little brook, until, further on, it suddenly entered the woods, where it laughed and gurgled and tumbled headlong over rocks in the most delightful way.

Halfway towards the woods, Alec halted. ‚ÄúWait a bit, Elizabeth,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúand I‚Äôll cut back to the house and get Norah to put us up some lunch.‚Äù

‚ÄúAll right,‚Äù Blue Bonnet agreed, sitting down in the long meadow-grass to wait. The three dogs had disappeared on an important chase, and she was left all alone. From where she sat there was nothing to be seen but open fields and blue sky; and these sent her thoughts homeward. She had been two weeks in Woodford. Looking back now, they seemed to have been rather long weeks. She had spent so much of them indoors, and there had been so many things to be done, to be learned.

Lying on her back in the tall grass, Blue Bonnet tried to imagine herself back on the prairie. She forgot that she hated the prairie. Oh, but it was good to be out in the open air and sunshine, doing nothing, wanting nothing, caring for nothing!

Alec‚Äôs halloa brought her back to the present. He came up at a quick pace, a small covered basket in his hand. ‚ÄúWas I very long?‚Äù he asked.

41 ‚ÄúLong enough for me to get to Texas and back.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôd like to have made the trip with you.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet had scrambled to her feet. ‚ÄúI think I shall come out here every day for a whole hour and do nothing,‚Äù she said.

‚ÄúI do nothing every day at home—for more than an hour,‚Äù Alec answered. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs pretty slow work sometimes.‚Äù

They had reached the woods now, the brook a slender, noisy thread beside them. On and on they followed it; now on this side, now on that; talking, laughing, growing better acquainted every moment. Ahead of them, the three dogs raced and barked and behaved in the absurd, carefree way usual with puppies.

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt Solomon getting better-looking every day?‚Äù Blue Bonnet said.

‚ÄúIs he? He must have been a beauty at the start,‚Äù Alec declared.

‚ÄúOh, he isn‚Äôt a thoroughbred—except as to his feelings; but he‚Äôs a mighty nice dog. He‚Äôs devoted to Aunt Lucinda.‚Äù

‚ÄúDoes she return his devotion?‚Äù

‚ÄúI honestly think she does like him a little; and she really is good to him,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said, soberly.

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs having the time of his life now, all right,‚Äù Alec laughed. A moment later he came to a sudden halt; he had been fighting against the need for42 rest for the last half-hour. It was intolerable to be played out in this way, with Blue Bonnet showing not the slightest sign of fatigue.

‚ÄúWe might camp here,‚Äù he suggested. In spite of himself, he could not keep the tiredness out of his voice.

Blue Bonnet looked up at him. ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù she said quickly, ‚Äúthis will be fine.‚Äù

They spread the napkin covering the basket over a flat stone and laid out the lunch.

‚ÄúMy, but I‚Äôm hungry,‚Äù Blue Bonnet declared. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs fun, isn‚Äôt it, eating out-of-doors?‚Äù

Alec nodded.

‚ÄúI‚Äôm having a tea-party this afternoon,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said. ‚ÄúJust a lot of girls, or you should have been invited.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm afraid I don‚Äôt like tea-parties,‚Äù Alec laughed.

‚ÄúThis is my first. I think it‚Äôs going to be lots of fun; only I‚Äôm scared I sha‚Äôn‚Äôt do Aunt Lucinda credit.‚Äù

‚ÄúThere isn‚Äôt anything to do, except put on your best duds and act ‚Äòproper.‚Äô‚Äù

Blue Bonnet took a second sandwich. ‚ÄúBut acting ‚Äòproper‚Äô in Woodford seems to mean such a lot.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat time does the shindig come off?‚Äù

‚ÄúHalf-past five. Sarah Blake‚Äôs coming, and Kitty Clark, Amanda Parker, Debby Slade, and43 Ruth and Susy Doyle. I know Sarah and Debby; they‚Äôve called. There are a lot of girls in Woodford, aren‚Äôt they?‚Äù

‚ÄúLoads. And I‚Äôll bet my best hat that not a single one of them, if they had a tea-party on, would be off tramping the woods like this,‚Äù Alec said, passing the apple turnovers and cheese.

‚ÄúBut it isn‚Äôt until afternoon!‚Äù Blue Bonnet exclaimed. ‚ÄúOh, Alec, think how nearly summer is over! School‚Äôll be beginning soon now. It‚Äôs going to be odd, having a woman teacher; I‚Äôve always studied under tutors. I‚Äôve had a lot of different ones. Aunt Lucinda says that largely accounts for my ‚Äòdesultory habits.‚Äô But I‚Äôve read a good deal. Uncle Cliff used to have a box of books sent out every little while. I haven‚Äôt kept up my music very well—all of the tutors weren‚Äôt musical. I can play by ear, though; but Aunt Lucinda says it would be better if I didn‚Äôt.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat makes you quote Miss Clyde so much?‚Äù Alec asked.

Blue Bonnet laughed. ‚ÄúBecause it seems somehow as if it were Aunt Lucinda who was running this ranch.‚Äù She leaned back against a gnarled old stump. ‚ÄúSometimes I wish,‚Äù she said, ‚Äúthat there were two of me—so that one of us could stay at home and be taught things, and behave nicely, while the other went wandering about as she liked.‚Äù

44 ‚ÄúYou might adopt Sarah for your alter ego,‚Äù Alec suggested.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs very puzzling—how people get mixed up. ‚ÄúI‚Äôve never tried it; sometimes I‚Äôve thought it seemed rather lonesome being in a family where there weren‚Äôt any women.‚Äù Alec commenced to gather up the dishes, tossing the scraps to the dogs.

Blue Bonnet‚Äôs eyes were thoughtful. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs strange how much we have in common. Oh, Alec, I ought to be doing that!‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs all done,‚Äù Alec answered.

‚ÄúSarah would‚Äôve?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes, and washed the dishes in the brook, and tidied things up generally.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut at home no one ever expected me to do anything like that,‚Äù Blue Bonnet explained; ‚Äúthat‚Äôs the reason I‚Äôm always forgetting now.‚Äù

The talk drifted from Texas to Woodford and back again; broken by long pauses, in which each was content to sit silent in the soft green twilight of the woods, listening to the faint rustling of the trees overhead, the murmuring of the brook, and the occasional call of a bird.

45 It was a good while before Alec looked at his watch; then he sprang to his feet. ‚ÄúElizabeth, you‚Äôve got exactly one hour and a half in which to make a two hour and a half walk, and get into your company duds.‚Äù

‚ÄúThree minutes after—now!‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd they don‚Äôt even know where I am!‚Äù Blue Bonnet gasped.

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll have to do some pretty tall sprinting,‚Äù Alec said.

It seemed to Blue Bonnet that after miles of hurried, heated scrambling they were still fathoms deep in those interminable woods. She felt that Alec was hurrying far beyond his strength; but he would not let her go on without him. She had given up counting the numbers of times she had stepped into the brook instead of over it, and the tears in her skirt.

Then at last, rounding a sharp curve, they saw the open meadow before them. They were crossing it when Alec held up his hand. ‚ÄúListen!‚Äù he said.

Faint and clear through the summer stillness sounded the village clock, striking half-past five.

Suddenly the humor of the situation struck Blue Bonnet. ‚ÄúMy first tea-party!‚Äù she gasped, between paroxysms of laughter.

‚ÄúCome on,‚Äù Alec warned her. ‚ÄúThere‚Äôs some46 one watching for you now down at the gate; probably there are scouts out in every direction.‚Äù

The watcher was Delia, the second girl. ‚ÄúOh, Miss Elizabeth,‚Äù she cried, ‚Äúwe‚Äôve been looking for you everywhere!‚Äù

At the back door, Miss Clyde met Blue Bonnet. ‚ÄúElizabeth!‚Äù she exclaimed, in tones of mingled relief and displeasure, ‚Äúwhere have you been?‚Äù

‚ÄúFollowing a brook with Alec, Aunt Lucinda.‚Äù

‚ÄúWith your guests waiting in the parlor, and tea-time set for half-past five! Go up to your room at once—I have laid out your things—we will talk of this later.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet stumbled blindly upstairs; sitting on the floor to change her shoes and stockings, she could hardly see the lacings for the tears blinding her eyes.

Everything went wrong; strings went into knots; pins pricked her. Worst of all, her heavy hair got into a hopeless tangle. She was struggling with it desperately, trying to get out the bits of twigs and dried moss, when someone, coming up behind her, took the brush from her hands. ‚ÄúLet me try, Elizabeth,‚Äù Mrs. Clyde said.

Soon, as if by magic, the soft thick braid was ready for its white ribbon. And all the time Mrs. Clyde had not spoken again, but the look in her eyes was harder to meet than Aunt Lucinda‚Äôs displeasure had been.

47 ‚ÄúHave I been very bad, Grandmother?‚Äù the girl asked, wistfully.

‚ÄúI cannot say that you have been very considerate, Elizabeth.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet‚Äôs lips quivered. Mrs. Clyde gave a few finishing touches to her white dress and hurried her downstairs.

And all this time, in the big front parlor, six highly-starched, immaculate young people were trying to appear interested in the decidedly perfunctory conversation Miss Clyde was endeavoring to keep up; carrying on among themselves at the same time little whispered exclamations of wonder and amusement.

Astonishment that anyone belonging to Miss Clyde could behave in such a way was only rivalled by the delightful uncertainty as to what might be to follow; and when presently Blue Bonnet, flushed, apologetic, but extremely glad to see them all, made her appearance, they received her warmly, if a little shyly.

In spite of its disastrous beginning, that tea-party was a great success,—a success due principally to Blue Bonnet herself. There was nothing stiff or formal about her; and her frank enjoyment of the society of so many girls of her own age was infectious.

Tea in Woodford was usually followed by music; and those of the girls who could play had come duly48 prepared. One by one, various old standbys were rendered, and then it was Blue Bonnet‚Äôs turn.

There was a laugh in the girl‚Äôs eyes as she took her place at the piano. A moment later, not a girl in the room but was beating time to the gay little tune she was playing.

Never before had such rollicking, joyous strains sounded through the sober old house. Mrs. Clyde, sitting by herself on the piazza, tapped the arm of her chair with her fan softly.

‚ÄúI got that from one of the cowboys,‚Äù Blue Bonnet turned to explain; ‚Äúyou ought to hear him play it on his fiddle, and see the others dancing, and the camp-fire glowing.‚Äù

Six pairs of eyes were fixed on Blue Bonnet. ‚ÄúOh,‚Äù Kitty cried, breathlessly, ‚Äúhow could you ever bear to come and leave it?—the ranch, I mean.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet‚Äôs face sobered. ‚ÄúBecause—‚Äù

‚ÄúShe had to come to go to school,‚Äù Debby Slade said.

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered, ‚ÄúI had to come.‚Äù

It was Sarah who made the first move to go, making it very prettily and very properly.

Blue Bonnet promptly vetoed the suggestion; they would all go out on the piazza and sing songs and tell stories in the moonlight.

But Sarah could be adamant when it was a case49 of duty; and Sarah‚Äôs ideas on duty were far-reaching. She was the eldest, and she felt that it was her place to set the example.

So, although some of her flock threatened to prove rebellious, she presently led them upstairs to the best bedroom, to put on hats and gloves.

Blue Bonnet, perched insecurely on the footboard of the big mahogany bedstead, beamed upon them one and all, urging them to drop in whenever they liked without waiting to be invited.

‚ÄúI will, for one,‚Äù Kitty promised; and, while the rest filed solemnly downstairs in line, Kitty pulled Blue Bonnet back, giving her a hearty hug. ‚ÄúOh, but I am glad you‚Äôve come!‚Äù she said.

Woodford etiquette required that Blue Bonnet should go with her guests to the front door—and no further. Blue Bonnet went gaily down to the gate.

On her way back to the house, she suddenly remembered her escapade of the afternoon, and what Aunt Lucinda had said. Perhaps Aunt Lucinda had forgotten by now.

One glance at Miss Clyde‚Äôs face, on re-entering the parlor, dispelled any such hope. Blue Bonnet took sudden heart of grace.

‚ÄúAunt Lucinda,‚Äù she said, going up to where her aunt stood waiting for her, ‚Äúit was a very nice party, and I‚Äôm very much obliged to you, and I—I am sorry I was late, I—‚Äù

50 ‚ÄúYou should not have gone at all, Elizabeth,‚Äù Miss Clyde said gravely.

The reproof which followed, if a little severe, was not unjust. Blue Bonnet listened silently, but her face expressed both astonishment and indignation. Never before had she been talked to in that fashion—and after she had said she was sorry, too. Her one desire was to get away.

‚ÄúIs that all, Aunt Lucinda?‚Äù she asked, the instant Miss Clyde stopped speaking.

‚ÄúThat is all, Elizabeth, except,‚Äù Miss Clyde‚Äôs voice softened a little, ‚Äúthat I very much regret having had to speak to you like this and that I hope it need not occur again. You may go now. Good night, Elizabeth.‚Äù

‚ÄúGood night, Aunt Lucinda,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered steadily; but, once on the other side of the parlor door, her breath caught in a quick sob, and later, as she buried her wet face in her pillow, she told herself miserably that she never, never could live up to Aunt Lucinda‚Äôs requirements.

CHAPTER IV
SCHOOL

Blue Bonnet came down to breakfast the next morning considerably less debonair than usual.

‚ÄúAnd how do you like tea-parties, Elizabeth?‚Äù her grandmother asked.

‚ÄúVery well, Grandmother. And I like the girls, all of them.‚Äù

Breakfast over, Blue Bonnet went upstairs to put her room in order. It was a task for which habit was by no means bringing any liking, and which had frequently to be done over. To-day, however, bureau drawers were closed, rugs straightened, and the bedclothes put on most carefully. Aunt Lucinda should find nothing to complain of that morning.

Miss Clyde, glancing in a little later, gave a nod of satisfaction; if only Elizabeth would do her best every day. ‚ÄúYour room looks very nice, Elizabeth,‚Äù she said, as Blue Bonnet came to do her Latin.

‚ÄúYes, Aunt Lucinda,‚Äù the girl said; ‚Äúare you ready now?‚Äù

‚ÄúI imagine Elizabeth has not been used to plain speaking.‚Äù

53 And Blue Bonnet did hurry, tearing headlong across the lawn to the stile, Solomon barking at her heels.

Blue Bonnet enjoyed her afternoon immensely; she had never dreamed Aunt Lucinda could be so—well, lovely.

55 ‚ÄúThere will not be time before church, Elizabeth,‚Äù Miss Clyde told her. ‚ÄúYou will have to hurry, as it is.‚Äù

58 The sunlight, shining through the rich, softly glowing colors, brought into relief the figure of The Good Shepherd with the lamb in his arms. ‚ÄúThe King of love my Shepherd is,

61 ‚ÄúSolomon,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said, ‚ÄúI reckon you‚Äôd better be going back now.‚Äù

Solomon cocked a protesting ear; he was quite content to sit there on the piazza steps and view the landscape. Solomon was a sociable dog and, though fond of Denham, thoroughly enjoyed being in company. Most of all, he enjoyed being wherever Blue Bonnet was.

‚ÄúSolomon!‚Äù Blue Bonnet said warningly.

Solomon rolled over on his back, waving his feet in the air; from the corner of one eye he watched to see what would happen next.

Leaning over, Blue Bonnet cuffed him lightly but firmly—which was hardly what Solomon had been looking for.

‚ÄúSolomon, I told you to go,‚Äù his mistress said; and Solomon went.

‚ÄúHe minds pretty well, don‚Äôt you think?‚Äù Blue Bonnet asked. ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt believe he‚Äôs ever had to mind before he came here, and it comes a bit hard; but he‚Äôs got a lot of sense, and when he once understands that he—‚Äù Blue Bonnet stopped speaking rather abruptly, as her eyes met her grandmother‚Äôs. Jumping up, she went indoors.

A moment later, from the parlor came the plaintive sound of an old Spanish melody, that chimed in well with the softly gathering twilight.

‚ÄúElizabeth has her mother‚Äôs touch,‚Äù Mrs. Clyde said.

62 ‚ÄúYes,‚Äù her daughter answered. Blue Bonnet‚Äôs mother had been very dear to the graver, older sister. Blue Bonnet looked up at the old red brick Academy, half in curiosity, half in dismay. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs not very—cheerful-looking, is it, Aunt Lucinda? Did you like going to school here?‚Äù

Blue Bonnet followed her aunt upstairs, with heart beating faster than usual. Here and there,63 through open doors, she caught glimpses of different classrooms. Should she have to sit at one of those little cramped-up desks?

Presently, Miss Clyde stopped before a glass door, on which was printed in large black letters, ‚ÄúPrincipal‚Äôs Office.‚Äù A moment later, Blue Bonnet was being presented to a tall, scholarly looking man who spoke to her very pleasantly, hoping she would enjoy her school life in Woodford.

‚ÄúI understand from your aunt that you have never been to school, Miss Elizabeth,‚Äù he added.

Blue Bonnet stood her examination very well. She had a bright intelligent mind; and her instruction, though not at all systematic according to Miss Clyde‚Äôs ideas, had been fairly thorough. In some of her studies, those she liked best, she was ahead of most girls of her age, and the daily drill her aunt had given her the past three weeks had proved most beneficial.

She came home that afternoon, jubilant. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm in Kitty‚Äôs class, Grandmother,‚Äù she announced, delightedly.64 ‚ÄúAll of us tea-party girls are in the same class. The teacher‚Äôs name is Miss Rankin. I‚Äôm afraid she looks rather determined.‚Äù

For the first few days Blue Bonnet enjoyed the novelty of school life thoroughly. Her classmates found her delightfully amusing, more so than her teacher did. She was so frankly astonished over all the little rulings of the classroom. ‚ÄúWhat a lot of things there are to remember!‚Äù she told Kitty.

By the middle of the second week, the unaccustomed drill and routine had become monotonous.

CHAPTER V
AN INVITATION

Uncle Joe came around to the front veranda, where Mr. Ashe sat looking rather lonely. ‚ÄúAny news from Boston and vicinity in that there mail?‚Äù he asked.

Mr. Ashe handed him Blue Bonnet‚Äôs latest letter.

‚ÄúHm, she don‚Äôt run much to length, does she?‚Äù Uncle Joe commented. ‚ÄúSo she‚Äôs going to school—and wishes schoolrooms were built without walls. Aunt Lucinda‚Äôs very kind, but Grandmother‚Äôs a darling. My lady can get a lot of meaning into a few words, can‚Äôt she, Cliff?‚Äù

But it was the postscript which gave Uncle Joe most delight.

‚ÄúI suppose,‚Äù Blue Bonnet had written, ‚Äúit‚Äôs on account of everything being so different that I keep thinking of the ranch. Anyhow, I think you might write me more about it, Uncle Cliff.‚Äù

‚ÄúSo, my lady!‚Äù Uncle Joe chuckled.

‚ÄúShe seems fairly contented,‚Äù Mr. Ashe said.

Uncle Joe grunted something unintelligible.

‚ÄúAt least, she doesn‚Äôt say anything about wanting to come back,‚Äù Mr. Ashe went on.

‚ÄúI‚Äôve heard before that the whole point of a69 woman‚Äôs letter was pretty apt to lay in the postscript,‚Äù Uncle Joe remarked; ‚Äúand I reckon this ain‚Äôt any exception to the rule. Meanwhile, the ‚Äúspunky little piece‚Äù was curled up comfortably in a big armchair at one side of the fireplace in the Trent library. Opposite her sat Alec, flushed and hoarse from a cold, but otherwise quite contented. Between the two, Bob, Ben, and Solomon sprawled in lazy comfort.

Outside, the September wind drove a fierce rain against the windows, making the warmth and brightness within doubly pleasant.

The Trent household, being, with the exception of Norah, a purely masculine establishment, was in Blue Bonnet‚Äôs eyes a delightful place. ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs so nice and untidyish,‚Äù she said now, looking about the pleasantly littered room.

‚ÄúSo am I.‚Äù

‚ÄúWas what?‚Äù

‚Äú‚ÄòTea day,‚Äô‚Äù Blue Bonnet repeated. ‚ÄúYou see, every one of those six girls was bound to ask me back in turn, and return; they‚Äôre all over now but one. CHAPTER VII
THE CLIMAX

Blue Bonnet‚Äôs suggestion regarding Mrs. Prior did not win favor with her mates; one or two of them agreed with Sarah that it would be ‚Äúnice, but—‚Äù and after a few fierce protests she let the matter drop.

It was a glorious Autumn, with sharp, stinging nights and mornings, and warm, hazy days. Blue Bonnet spent every available moment—not to mention a good many of the other kind—out-of-doors. And every day, the girl‚Äôs thoughts were more and more of the Blue Bonnet Ranch. All unconsciously, the longing to be back on it, to be leading again the old, careless, carefree life, crept into her letters,—bringing much joy to Uncle Cliff, and making Uncle Joe shake his head delightedly.

Not that her days in Woodford were not, in the main, happy ones. She had a knack of getting a good share of all the fun there was going. And there was a good deal going, off and on.

Blue Bonnet studied hard that evening. She meant to have good lessons on the morrow; she would go to Miss Rankin the first thing in the morning.

CHAPTER VIII
MR. HUNT

Mrs. Clyde, sitting at her sewing in her own room, started in surprise as the front door was slammed violently, followed by a quick rush of feet on the stairs.

That the commotion could only be caused by Elizabeth was probable, but what was she doing home from school at this hour?

Back to the ranch, where there were no explanations to be made; no Miss Rankins to be displeased with one; no principals to be sent to. 124 Grandmother listened to the long, rather incoherent story in a silence that Blue Bonnet did not feel to be entirely condemnatory. When Blue Bonnet went up to bed that evening, she found a little bundle of letters, smelling of lavender, lying on her dressing-table.

Her first thought was to sit down and read them then and there; but, with a little resolute shake of the head, she made herself get quite ready for bed136 first; then, wrapping a gaily striped Mexican blanket about her, she curled herself up on the foot of her bed, the letters in her lap.

And so vivid were they, so dear and familiar the scenes they portrayed, that presently the girl had forgotten time and place, and was feeling the prairie wind on her face; seeing the swaying of the tall grass; hearing the sounds of the ranch life—rejoicing in the freedom of it all.

161

CHAPTER X
UNCLE CLIFF

It was a rough ride, the narrow down-hill road turning abruptly more than once; then came a short cut across country through seldom-used lanes, with a field to cross before reaching the broad mill road.

At first, Victor was disposed to repent his sudden yielding; disposed to display that repentance very actively. And then Victor realized that the hand on the bridle rein was firm and steady—the hand of the master; and that his rider, if only a girl, knew how to ride.

And all the way, above the hurry and excitement, above her anxiety for Alec, one thought rang triumphantly through Blue Bonnet‚Äôs mind—she was not afraid.

Dr. Clark, gathering up the reins, preparatory to leaving Nesbit‚Äôs, saw the hurrying horse and waited. Ten chances to one, he was wanted.

‚ÄúWell!‚Äù he exclaimed, as Blue Bonnet drew up beside the gig, ‚Äúany of you girls come a cropper?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs Alec, Dr. Clark!‚Äù Slipping out of the saddle, Blue Bonnet told her errand. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll go back162 with you,‚Äù she added. ‚ÄúVictor‚Äôs had pretty hard service this afternoon; I‚Äôll leave him here for some one to look after him, and take him home by and by.‚Äù

‚ÄúWell, Miss Elizabeth, you surely can ride!‚Äù the doctor said, as Blue Bonnet climbed in beside him; and he marvelled over the sudden lighting up of her blue eyes.

Kitty was watching anxiously for them, ‚ÄúAlec seems some better, papa,‚Äù she said; ‚ÄúI am glad you‚Äôve come.‚Äù

The next day, Mr. Ashe spoke to Blue Bonnet about going home. CHAPTER XI
MY LADY BOUNTIFUL

But the return trip was not to be made yet; there was Thanksgiving—only a matter of days now—to come first, not to mention Christmas.

‚ÄúA real New England Thanksgiving!‚Äù Blue Bonnet checked the words off on her fingers. ‚ÄúI‚Äôve never had one of that kind, have I? The Boston relatives are coming! I‚Äôm rather scared of the Boston relatives; I‚Äôve an idea they‚Äôll be rather like Aunt Lucinda—only more so.‚Äù

The thought of those mid-year examinations was giving Blue Bonnet a good deal of uneasiness; she had found out that most decidedly she did not want her class to go on without her. And promotion would not altogether depend upon the result of the examinations, either; the regular class record counted for much—and she had done so poorly all the fall!

She needed little reminding to get at her studies these evenings, shutting herself up alone in the back parlor with a fortitude that Aunt Lucinda found most encouraging, and Mr. Ashe inwardly deplored. And then Grandmother said, slowly, ‚ÄúAll the same, Blue Bonnet—though I agree with you that there would hardly be time, under present circumstances, for you to get the shawl done, I do not at all approve of your taking things up and then dropping them as suddenly.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet looked into the fire; she had been afraid Grandmother would take it like that. Then she looked up, with eyes full of sudden mischief. ‚ÄúGrandmother, dear, I give you my word of honor, that the next time I start in to make anyone a crocheted shawl I‚Äôll finish it!‚Äù

And even Aunt Lucinda was obliged to smile.

Never days went by more quickly than those short December ones. And never, in Blue Bonnet‚Äôs experience, had days been half so full of business.

Two or three times a week came messages from Uncle Cliff, generally accompanied by packages for the box, or rather boxes. For Mr. Ashe had been243 promptly told of that second Christmas box, also destined for Texas, and had as promptly expressed his unqualified approval.

The two stood side by side on the table in the clubroom, and in one a big bundle of bright purple and crimson wools held no inconspicuous place.

There were shopping trips in town with Grandmother and Aunt Lucinda, and one made by the club in a body. Blue Bonnet declared she would never forget that shopping trip; Sarah inwardly registered the same vow, though from different reasons.

There were innumerable impromptu meetings of the club at the house of one or another.

There were the daily walks, which, now that the riding was over, Grandmother firmly insisted on.

And in between times were snatches of extra studying, hasty reviews.

‚ÄúAnd you‚Äôve gone through with it all every year for ages and ages!‚Äù Blue Bonnet said one morning, looking from Sarah to Kitty in positive admiration.

‚ÄúWhy don‚Äôt you put it centuries?‚Äù Kitty asked.

‚ÄúOf course we have,‚Äù Sarah said, calmly. She expected to pass; she always had, though never brilliantly; and when she went to bed on Christmas Eve, though it might be late, it would be with the comfortable feeling that she had accomplished all she had set out to do.

‚ÄúAunt Lucinda suggested that, too,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said to herself, sleepily. Christmas was the dearest time in all the year,—she had always known that,—but this year she was finding out its wonderful possibilities more clearly every day.

Two or three days later those dreadful examinations began, and like a good many other things in this world, proved upon closer acquaintance not half so dreadful as they had seemed, viewed at long distance.

‚ÄúI‚Äôm getting all the questions that I know,‚Äù Blue Bonnet rejoiced more than once; but for all her rejoicing, she walked softly those days.

CHAPTER XIV
CHRISTMAS

Aunt Lucinda was playing Christmas carols; it seemed to Blue Bonnet, listening in her big chair by one of the long windows, that the air had been full of carols all day. At church in the morning, at Sunday school in the afternoon; and later, as she and Grandmother made their rounds in the big, old-fashioned sleigh, carrying Christmas cheer to more than one home, the very bells had seemed to be singing a carol of their own.

The little bank had been emptied of its contents the morning before, considerably more coming out than Blue Bonnet herself had put in, though she had been faithful in those weekly contributions; and she and Uncle Cliff had spent a delightful hour in a little toyshop, rather off the main stream of traffic—chosen because it was little and looked sort of lonely and forlorn, whose proprietor had been most sincere in his urgent request that they should call again.

That long day in Boston,—with the blessed knowledge at the back of one‚Äôs mind that one had ‚Äúpassed,‚Äù and that school was done with for ten whole days; with the wind nipping one‚Äôs fingertips249 and reddening one‚Äôs cheeks; with the stores reminding one of the fairy-land, and the streets almost as gay and wonderful as the stores; with Uncle Cliff declaring that Christmas only came once a year, and that this was the first time they had ever had a chance to go shopping together properly,—had been a day not soon to be forgotten.

And then the making up of the baskets in the evening! Grandmother insisted that one sleigh would never carry them all.

‚ÄúEvery part of Christmas seems the nicest,‚Äù Blue Bonnet had sighed, happily, filling a bag with nuts and raisins for the small Pattersons, and almost envying Luella Patterson the brown-eyed, brown-haired doll lying smiling up at her from its box.

Nor had this ‚Äúbetween-time‚Äù Sunday lacked its own particular charm. ‚ÄúIt gives one a little chance to get one‚Äôs breath,‚Äù Blue Bonnet confided to Solomon, curled up in the chair beside her, ‚ÄúThough it hasn‚Äôt been what one would call precisely an idle day! But I‚Äôve got everything ready—think of that, Solomon! All the home things packed away in the closet, and after supper, Uncle Cliff and I are going to take Alec‚Äôs and the ‚ÄòWe are Seven‚Äô theirs. Think what a lot of presents I‚Äôve had to wrap up and write on!‚Äù

Solomon wriggled appreciatively; there was something for him,—he had been told so.

250 While out in the hall stood a big, travel-stained box, object of Solomon‚Äôs liveliest curiosity. It had arrived the day before from Texas.

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you want to come sing this, Blue Bonnet?‚Äù Aunt Lucinda asked; and as Blue Bonnet came to the piano, she struck the opening chords of Mrs. Clyde‚Äôs favorite carol: ‚ÄúO Little Town of Bethlehem.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet sang it all, looking out to where above the familiar street the silent stars went by, and trying to picture to herself the little hillside town of Bethlehem, resting in its quiet sleep.

‚Äú‚ÄòO holy Child of Bethlehem!

The great, glad tidings tell

The girl‚Äôs clear voice sounded softly through the quiet parlor, with its trimmings of evergreen and holly, carrying two of her listeners back to more than one Christmas Eve in the past.

All in all, Christmas Eve was almost as nice as Christmas itself, Blue Bonnet decided that night, sitting on the hearth-rug before the fire in her own room. Then her face grew suddenly wistful. It was not so many years ago that her mother had251 sat on this same hearth-rug, thinking of the joys to come on the morrow, while the clock on the mantel ticked away the moments bringing the great day of days nearer and nearer.

Solomon was the first to give her Christmas greeting the next morning, choosing Christmas for his first venture above stairs before breakfast; aided and abetted therein by Delia. Sure, and the child should have somebody to talk to on Christmas morning—and Solomon was wiser than a deal of humans.

He received warm welcome; Blue Bonnet was sitting up in bed, a little square, pasteboard box in her hand. ‚ÄúI found it under my pillow,‚Äù she told the ever-curious Solomon. ‚ÄúNow how did Grandmother smuggle it in without my knowing it?‚Äù

She slipped the slender gold band with its one deep, dark blue stone on her finger. ‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt it pretty, Solomon?‚Äù

And it was with the brightest of Christmas faces that Blue Bonnet came down to breakfast half an hour later. No one was in the dining-room, but the table stood ready, a true Christmas table, with its shining silver and bowl of crimson roses; its pile of presents at each place; overflowing, in Blue Bonnet‚Äôs case, from table to floor.

‚ÄúPlease!‚Äù—Blue Bonnet went to the door—‚ÄúWon‚Äôt everybody hurry! I don‚Äôt think I can wait much longer!‚Äù

252 ‚ÄúSo hungry as all that, Honey?‚Äù her uncle laughed, coming in from his morning constitutional on the veranda. ‚ÄúMerry Christmas!‚Äù

The line was forming for the march out to supper; once in the dining-room, it broke up into little groups, four to a table.

And then, from every side came eager exclamations of surprise and pleasure; for in the center of each table was a little candle-lighted Christmas tree, from the base of which ran four crimson ribbons, to which were attached the place cards, with their borders of Christmas elves bearing dainty sprays of holly and mistletoe; while among the decorations on the trees were tiny favors, both pretty and amusing.

It was all as much a surprise to Blue Bonnet as to her guests; she had known that Miss Lucinda was giving considerable thought to the details of her party, but she had never dreamed of anything266 like this. Blue Bonnet told herself, that she never, never would be vexed or impatient with Aunt Lucinda again—let her seem ever so exacting.

If it would only go on and on indefinitely! ‚ÄúWhy must all the nicest things come to an end so soon?‚Äù Blue Bonnet asked her partner abruptly.

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs just what I‚Äôm complaining of! There ought to be more than sixty minutes to an hour—at times like these.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut, Miss Blue Bonnet, think what confusion—‚Äù

‚ÄúYou know—‚Äù Blue Bonnet‚Äôs eyes were most demure, ‚Äúwe really manage little things like that much better out in Texas.‚Äù

‚ÄúI think we may call it a perfect success from start to finish,‚Äù Miss Lucinda said.

CHAPTER XV
A DARE

Monday morning, Mr. Ashe left for the West; and the next day, the new term began.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôll seem odd, not going to Miss Rankin‚Äôs room,‚Äù Blue Bonnet said, overtaking Debby on the way to school. ‚ÄúI wonder if she‚Äôll miss us.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet came home that afternoon most enthusiastic; Miss Fellows was all she ought to be, she shouldn‚Äôt have a bit of trouble with her.

‚ÄúAnd does the lady in question feel confident regarding you?‚Äù Mrs. Clyde asked.

Blue Bonnet dropped her strap of books, ran for her skates, paid a visit to the cookie jar in the pantry, patted Solomon, and with a ‚ÄúGood-bye, Grandmother,‚Äù was off, leaving Mrs. Clyde feeling as if a small whirlwind had swept through the quiet house.

What with school, her afternoons on the pond, her evenings of study, broken by occasional neighborhood gatherings, Blue Bonnet found the time slipping by very fast. While she missed her uncle greatly, she was learning more and more how much can be done by letter-writing, and those were far from doleful letters that traveled every week from Woodford to the far-away Texas ranch.

The weather held wonderfully; never had the pond been in better condition than during those January days.

‚ÄúBut the thaw‚Äôs bound to come before long,‚Äù Debby predicted one afternoon.

271 ‚ÄúBlue Bonnet!‚Äù Susy gasped.

272 But the boys were far down at the other end by now, fighting an exciting game to a finish. The pond had been thinning rapidly the last half hour, for, with the coming of night, a cold wind had sprung up.

At first, Blue Bonnet thoroughly enjoyed that swift rush along through the gathering dusk; they had the wind at their back, and ahead of them the pond to themselves. Then the two hours or more already spent in skating that afternoon began to tell on her, and with the sense of fast-growing fatigue came equally rapid misgivings. She glanced sideways at her companion; why wouldn‚Äôt Kitty speak! If only she would admit the foolishness of the undertaking, Blue Bonnet would give in too, but until Kitty gave in—she would not.

Kitty was thinking the same; she knew, as Blue Bonnet did not, not only the foolishness, but the risk of what they had undertaken. What had possessed her to start such a ball rolling? Once started, it went without saying that she could not be the first to throw up the game. Blue Bonnet was getting tired already, one could see that, though she was trying not to show it; and then—

But Kitty reckoned without knowledge.

273 The pond was growing narrower now, with sharp twists and turns that made Blue Bonnet think of the brook she and Alec had followed that August afternoon. The thought of the brook reminded her of Aunt Lucinda.

For just a moment, Blue Bonnet wavered; Aunt Lucinda had gone into town and would not be back until the nine o‚Äôclock train—Grandmother was alone, and would be worried.

‚ÄúI am ready whenever you are,‚Äù she answered; ‚Äúyou have only to say the word.‚Äù

‚ÄúI thought you wanted to go to the very end?‚Äù

So the two pushed doggedly on through the fast-deepening dusk, stumbling more than once against snags; tired, cold, hungry, and miserable, and with the discouraging knowledge that every moment was taking them further from home.

274 It seemed to Blue Bonnet as if the pond had no end, but was like some dreary, enchanted lake in the fairy stories; that she and Kitty, like the brook, must go on and on forever. It did not seem possible that it could be the same pond she and the others had skated on so gaily that afternoon—if it really was that afternoon.

It was quite dark by now. Far away, across the fields, a solitary light showed in some lonely farmhouse window, and now and then they caught the sound of a dog barking.

CHAPTER XVII
A CLASS AFFAIR

Kitty came down the class-room aisle as jubilant and beaming, as if, outside, March winds and March rains were not having it all their own way.

‚ÄúI‚Äôve my subject for the Sargent!‚Äù she announced to the little group gathered about one of the windows at the far end of the room.

It was Kitty who broke the short silence that followed. ‚ÄúBlue Bonnet Ashe, do you mean that?‚Äù

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Blue Bonnet answered. She—would tell them why. She couldn‚Äôt bear to have them think her—not loyal.

‚ÄúMaybe,‚Äù Kitty‚Äôs gray eyes were full of scorn. ‚ÄúMaybe you have taken French longer than we have, but you certainly do not seem to have learned the meaning of ‚Äòesprit de corps‚Äô! Perhaps they don‚Äôt teach that sort of thing—out in Texas!‚Äù

It seemed as if noontime would never come; she hated the big, busy schoolroom and—everybody in it; at least, nearly everybody! Girls were—detestable. A boy wouldn‚Äôt have said a thing like that. If Uncle Cliff could know how mean Kitty had been. One thing was sure—they could never be friends again.

‚ÄúMy dear—‚Äù

Sarah would think it was her duty to be just the same as usual, but she didn‚Äôt want ‚Äúduty friendliness.‚Äù Good; Miss Fellows was going to ring for order right now.

Blue Bonnet was glad that drawing followed327 immediately; one didn‚Äôt have to answer questions in drawing, and there was a chance to think. Though in this case, thinking only meant going over and over the same old road and winding up each time at the same high, blank wall. Once, glancing up unexpectedly, she found Ruth looking at her in a wonder that was half reproach.

Blue Bonnet dropped her pencil on to the desk and turned to the window. Ruth loved law and order as she did not, and yet Ruth was prepared to act in open defiance of both, in obedience to that intangible something called ‚Äúclass spirit.‚Äù

Blue Bonnet stared at the soft, fleecy clouds piling themselves up like great, white snow-drifts. Was she wrong after all?

And then the clouds sent her thoughts back to that night on the pond, to the long, weary tramp afterwards through real snow-drifts. Was this, after all, another sort of dare? Were they—all those others, consciously or unconsciously, daring her now to break her promise?

But ‚Äúliving straight and true‚Äù could never mean breaking one‚Äôs word.

‚ÄúMiss Elizabeth!‚Äù the drawing-master laid a hand on her book; he intended criticizing rather sharply her work, or, rather, lack of work, but the face she turned towards him disarmed him.

328 ‚ÄúI beg your pardon, Mr. Post,‚Äù she answered.

Debby was signaling to her, doing it rather openly, too. Blue Bonnet shook her head, impatiently. Why wouldn‚Äôt they let her alone?

Five minutes later, she found herself out in the corridor with the other members of the French class. 329 A moment more and she had closed the door of the recitation-room behind her.

Monsieur was not there yet. From the open window came a sound of muffled laughter, suddenly hushed; the class had reached the yard.

Monsieur was coming now. Blue Bonnet went over to her usual place; it didn‚Äôt matter if he were cross, nothing mattered—now that she was really started along the dismal road leading to that dreary land called Coventry,—a land that in the old Texas days she had never dreamed of even sighting.

It was the shortest French recitation the old room had ever seen. And it is to be feared that even then the teacher did most of the ‚Äúconversing.‚Äù

330 When it was over, and they were leaving the room together, Mr. Hunt laid a hand for a moment on Blue Bonnet‚Äôs shoulder. ‚ÄúThey teach you how to keep promises out in your beloved Texas, it would seem,‚Äù he said.

Once at home, and there had been no tarrying along the way that afternoon, she made straight for her room. There Mrs. Clyde found her, lying face down on the bed, shaken with sobs, while a much distressed small dog did his best to console her.

‚ÄúDisobeyed the rules now?‚Äù Grandmother suggested.

331 ‚ÄúGrandmother! Wouldn‚Äôt you have gone with your class?‚Äù

For a moment, Mrs. Clyde said nothing, there was a far-away look in her eyes; then she smiled softly. ‚ÄúI suppose I should have, because once I—did. But I had not promised. ‚ÄúDogs never go back on one.‚Äù Blue Bonnet gave Solomon an affectionate squeeze.

‚ÄúHalloa!‚Äù Alec called, cheerily, and Blue Bonnet,332 suddenly on the alert, could detect no change in his manner. But perhaps he didn‚Äôt know.

‚ÄúLook here,‚Äù he said, ‚ÄúI‚Äôve evolved a capital scheme—I think I shall take up the profession of furnishing ideas to the needy. CHAPTER XVIII
COVENTRY

It was after opening exercises on Monday morning, that Mr. Hunt, stepping to the front of the platform, announced that the pupils from Miss Fellows‚Äô room who had absented themselves from French on Friday afternoon, were to go to his office instead of to their classroom.

The assembly-room had been very still while the principal was speaking, but as he finished a little ripple of excitement ran over it, and here and there there was a curious turning of heads. Then Miss Rankin struck the preliminary chords, and the various classes formed into line.

Blue Bonnet, with Kitty just behind and Ruth only two places ahead, was wishing with all her heart that presently she too might drop out of line with the others. The fourteen had not been the only ones towards whom curious glances had been turned that morning. ‚ÄúThe girl who had not cut‚Äù was as much an object of interest as the pupils who had; only there had been no sympathy for her.

That she didn‚Äôt look as if she cared, was the general verdict; Alec, watching her from his corner of the big room, knew better. He would have334 liked to tell those girls what he thought of them—it was the girls who were the worst. He was glad when opening exercises were over and Blue Bonnet had reached the comparative shelter of her classroom.

She was glad, too, though for the moment, in spirit at least, she was in the office with the fourteen. What would Mr. Hunt say to them? Kitty had said once that he could be ‚Äúrather awful.‚Äù Perhaps Kitty had exaggerated; she had not found him so.

But the young people waiting in the office were not so hopeful.

‚ÄúI believe he‚Äôs just keeping us waiting on purpose!‚Äù Kitty grumbled, as the moments went by and Mr. Hunt did not appear.

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll lose our Latin,‚Äù Susy mourned.

‚ÄúIf that‚Äôs all we lose, we‚Äôll be mighty lucky,‚Äù one of the boys told her.

‚ÄúKit‚Äôs lost her temper already,‚Äù Billy Slade remarked.

‚ÄúWhy didn‚Äôt he tell us he was going to take the class Friday afternoon?‚Äù his sister Debby protested. ‚ÄúThen we should have been all right.‚Äù

‚ÄúHush! he‚Äôs coming!‚Äù one of the other girls warned.

‚ÄúGet out your hankys, young ladies!‚Äù Billy whispered. ‚ÄúTry and look as penitent as possible!‚Äù

335 ‚ÄúI won‚Äôt!‚Äù Kitty declared. ‚ÄúI‚Äôm not sorry, and I won‚Äôt say I am!‚Äù

‚ÄúYou will before he‚Äôs through with you, my young friend,‚Äù Billy retorted.

Kitty tossed her red head defiantly, but a moment later even her courage wavered at sight of Mr. Hunt‚Äôs face.

For a moment he said nothing. Then, sitting down at his desk, he put one or two direct questions to each in turn. After which followed another short silence, broken only by the ticking of the clock, and from a room below, the sound of children chanting their multiplication table in unison.

‚ÄúTwice two is four!‚Äù Debby found herself nervously repeating it with them under her breath. Would Mr. Hunt never speak!

She caught Susy‚Äôs eye; Susy was looking penitent enough to touch a heart of stone, Debby thought. So, for that matter, were most of the girls.

Debby began to realize that anything begun in haste might require repenting of at leisure.

And then Mr. Hunt pronounced sentence, prefacing it first with a few remarks, which, if brief, were none the less pointed.

He considered their recent conduct utterly inexcusable; it had involved not only a wilful and deliberate breaking of rules, but, in intention, great336 discourtesy and disrespect towards a gentleman who was a comparative stranger to them, and, in a sense, the guest of the class.

He should, therefore, suspend them in a body for one week; they could report to him, before school opened, next Monday morning; also, it being an implied condition that all competitors for the Sargent should be pupils in good standing, it was an open question whether or no they would have the right to try for it. He would decide upon that later. They were dismissed.

Out in the yard, fourteen very crestfallen young people looked at each other in dismay.

Not to be allowed to try for the Sargent! Each of the fourteen felt an immediate and strong conviction that he or she would have been among the prize winners.

To be suspended for a whole week!

Ruth mopped her eyes openly. Oh, dear, what would her mother and father say!

‚ÄúHe certainly can do things up brown, when he sets out to,‚Äù Billy commented, a rueful note underlying his chuckle.

Kitty stamped her foot. ‚ÄúIt isn‚Äôt fair! We had every right to do what we did—under the circumstances.‚Äù

‚ÄúExcept the right—to do it,‚Äù one of the boys commented.

‚ÄúHow everybody looks at us,‚Äù Hester sighed.337 ‚ÄúI suppose they‚Äôre wondering what we are all doing out of school at this time of the morning.‚Äù

‚ÄúProbably they think we‚Äôre delegates to something or other,‚Äù Billy remarked, ‚Äúchosen on account of good conduct.‚Äù

‚ÄúCut it!‚Äù one of his companions commanded.

‚ÄúWe did, once,‚Äù Debby laughed, ‚Äúbut we never will again.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt isn‚Äôt fair!‚Äù Kitty repeated; she hoped her father would see it in that light. ‚ÄúCome on home with me, Debby; at any rate, we sha‚Äôn‚Äôt have to study.‚Äù

‚ÄúAren‚Äôt you going to try and keep up with the class this week?‚Äù Hester asked.

Kitty shrugged. ‚ÄúMaybe—maybe not. I do wish Amanda Parker would go visiting for the week,‚Äù she confided to Debby, as they turned the corner together. ‚ÄúShe‚Äôll be mighty tiresome! She‚Äôs such an ‚ÄòI told you so‚Äô sort of girl.‚Äù

‚ÄúIsn‚Äôt it queer,‚Äù Debby said, ‚Äúthat Blue Bonnet, who dislikes school more than any of us do, hasn‚Äôt got to—‚Äù

‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you mention Blue Bonnet Ashe to me!‚Äù Kitty broke in. ‚ÄúHorrid little prig!‚Äù

‚ÄúYou know better, Kitty Clark!‚Äù

‚ÄúThen she‚Äôs a coward—and that‚Äôs even worse.‚Äù

‚ÄúAlec says he knows she had some good reason.‚Äù

‚ÄúNaturally! She has so much more to offer.‚Äù

Kitty slammed the front gate with an energy that brought her mother to the door. Mrs. Clark was something of an invalid, and her daughter had thought it as well not to trouble her with any account of Friday‚Äôs doings until she found out what the consequences were. ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know what your father will say!‚Äù she339 cried, eying Kitty in mingled amazement and dismay. Girls never did such things in her day.

But of course, all that was changed now. One could not be friends with a girl who—

Kitty gave the swing an impatient push. She was glad that she had not gone to the matin√©e with them on Saturday—though Alec had been mighty angry with her for holding out; Blue Bonnet should see that they were not all going to—

Kitty was not the only one of the fourteen to whom the thought of the classroom from which they had been exiled had grown suddenly very dear.

On the other hand, their fellow-pupils were giving no less thought to them. When recess came,340 and there was still no sign of them, excitement ran high, so did conjecture.

Blue Bonnet, standing alone quite at the lower end of the yard, wondered forlornly if all the recesses to come were to be like this? For the first time in her life, she had been cut, and by more than one schoolmate, and the experience had been far from pleasant.

Sarah, of them all, acted just as usual; but Sarah was—Sarah; Amanda was clearly on the fence—very well, she might stay there. Of her intimates among the French class, Ruth and Susy had been too absorbed in their own thoughts, during those few moments before school opened, to do more than say good morning. Debby had barely nodded, while Kitty had done neither.

It was Kitty‚Äôs attitude that hurt most. Alec had refused to give her Kitty‚Äôs reason for not accepting his invitation—as if she could not guess, and he had managed, for this time, to break down the sense of reserve and embarrassment between herself and the other girls. Besides, at the theatre one forgot other people.

But Sunday had not been easy; Blue Bonnet had come home from Sunday-school in hardly the state of mind her teacher—a gentle little body—would have rejoiced in. The talk with Grandmother in the twilight, and Aunt Lucinda‚Äôs few words of encouragement, had helped some.

341 But to-day! And there would be all of April and May, besides the rest of March and part of June, before school closed.

Blue Bonnet turned to watch a group of children; they were playing ‚ÄúThe farmer in the dell,‚Äù and Julia Blake beckoned invitingly to her to come make one of the big ring. Any of the little Blakes could have told you what a delightful playfellow Blue Bonnet was.

Sarah was crossing the yard towards her, while midway between Sarah and the open doors, Amanda halted, irresolutely.

‚ÄúOh, Blue Bonnet!‚Äù Sarah called.

‚ÄúDid you come because you wanted to, or because you didn‚Äôt want to?‚Äù
